<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Drake equation</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="hatnote" role="note">This article is about Frank Drake's equation. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Drake_equation_(disambiguation)" title="Drake equation (disambiguation)">Drake equation (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dr._Frank_Drake.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="724" data-file-width="646" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Dr._Frank_Drake.jpg/220px-Dr._Frank_Drake.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Dr._Frank_Drake.jpg/330px-Dr._Frank_Drake.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Dr._Frank_Drake.jpg/440px-Dr._Frank_Drake.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dr._Frank_Drake.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Dr. <a href="/wiki/Frank_Drake" title="Frank Drake">Frank Drake</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <b>Drake equation</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Probability_theory" title="Probability theory">probabilistic argument</a> used to arrive at an estimate of the number of active, communicative <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_life" title="Extraterrestrial life">extraterrestrial civilizations</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a> <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burchell_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Burchell-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> The number of such civilizations, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">N</span>, is assumed to be equal to the mathematical product of</p>
<ul>
<li>(i) the average rate of star formation, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">R</span>*, in our galaxy,</li>
<li>(ii) the fraction of formed stars, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">f<sub>p</sub></span>, that have planets,</li>
<li>(iii) the average number of planets per star, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">n<sub>e</sub></span>, that can potentially support life,</li>
<li>(iv) the fraction of those planets, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">f<sub>l</sub></span>, that actually develop life,</li>
<li>(v) the fraction of planets bearing life on which intelligent, civilized life, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">f<sub>i</sub></span>, has developed,</li>
<li>(vi) the fraction of these civilizations that have developed communications, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">f<sub>c</sub></span>, i.e., technologies that release detectable signs into space, and</li>
<li>(vii) the length of time, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">L</span>, over which such civilizations release detectable signals, for a combined expression of:</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>N</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<msup>
<mi>R</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>∗<!-- ∗ --></mo>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>⋅<!-- ⋅ --></mo>
<msub>
<mi>f</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>p</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>⋅<!-- ⋅ --></mo>
<msub>
<mi>n</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>e</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>⋅<!-- ⋅ --></mo>
<msub>
<mi>f</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>ℓ<!-- ℓ --></mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>⋅<!-- ⋅ --></mo>
<msub>
<mi>f</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>i</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>⋅<!-- ⋅ --></mo>
<msub>
<mi>f</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>c</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>⋅<!-- ⋅ --></mo>
<mi>L</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle N=R^{\ast }\cdot f_{p}\cdot n_{e}\cdot f_{\ell }\cdot f_{i}\cdot f_{c}\cdot L}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle N=R^{\ast }\cdot f_{p}\cdot n_{e}\cdot f_{\ell }\cdot f_{i}\cdot f_{c}\cdot L}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/41816b3d22b6ee19302273fb05a736408dc26877" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:30.51ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>The equation was written in 1961 by <a href="/wiki/Frank_Drake" title="Frank Drake">Frank Drake</a>, not for purposes of quantifying the number of civilizations, but as a way to stimulate scientific dialogue at the first scientific meeting on the <a href="/wiki/Search_for_extraterrestrial_intelligence" title="Search for extraterrestrial intelligence">search for intelligent extraterrestrial life</a> (SETI). <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-December_2002_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-December_2002-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> The equation summarizes the main concepts which scientists must contemplate when considering the question of other radio-communicative life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-December_2002_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-December_2002-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Criticism related to the Drake equation focuses not on the equation itself, but on the fact that the estimated values for several of its factors are highly conjectural, the combined effect being that the uncertainty associated with any derived value is so large that the equation cannot be used to draw firm conclusions.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Drake_equation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In September 1959, physicists <a href="/wiki/Giuseppe_Cocconi" title="Giuseppe Cocconi">Giuseppe Cocconi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philip_Morrison" title="Philip Morrison">Philip Morrison</a> published an article in the journal <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)">Nature</a></i> with the provocative title "Searching for Interstellar Communications."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cocconi_1959_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cocconi_1959-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-history-6">[6]</a></sup> Cocconi and Morrison argued that <a href="/wiki/Radio_telescope" title="Radio telescope">radio telescopes</a> had become sensitive enough to pick up transmissions that might be broadcast into space by civilizations orbiting other stars. Such messages, they suggested, might be transmitted at a <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelength</a> of 21 centimeters (1,420.4 <a href="/wiki/Hertz" title="Hertz">megahertz</a>). This is the wavelength of radio emission by neutral <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, the most common element in the universe, and they reasoned that other intelligences might see this as a logical landmark in the <a href="/wiki/Radio_spectrum" title="Radio spectrum">radio spectrum</a>.</p>
<p>Two months later, Harvard University astronomy professor <a href="/wiki/Harlow_Shapley" title="Harlow Shapley">Harlow Shapley</a> speculated on the number of inhabited planets in the universe, saying "The universe has 10 million, million, million suns (10 followed by 18 zeros) similar to our own. One in a million has planets around it. Only one in a million million has the right combination of chemicals, temperature, water, days and nights to support planetary life as we know it. This calculation arrives at the estimated figure of 100 million worlds where life has been forged by evolution."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SydneyMorningHerald_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-SydneyMorningHerald-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>Seven months after Cocconi and Morrison published their article, Drake made the first systematic search for signals from extraterrestrial intelligent beings. Using the 25 meter dish of the <a href="/wiki/National_Radio_Astronomy_Observatory" title="National Radio Astronomy Observatory">National Radio Astronomy Observatory</a> in <a href="/wiki/Green_Bank,_West_Virginia" title="Green Bank, West Virginia">Green Bank, West Virginia</a>, Drake monitored two nearby Sun-like stars: <a href="/wiki/Epsilon_Eridani" title="Epsilon Eridani">Epsilon Eridani</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tau_Ceti" title="Tau Ceti">Tau Ceti</a>. In this project, which he called <a href="/wiki/Project_Ozma" title="Project Ozma">Project Ozma</a>, he slowly scanned frequencies close to the 21 cm wavelength for six hours a day from April to July 1960.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-history-6">[6]</a></sup> The project was well designed, inexpensive, and simple by today's standards. It was also unsuccessful.</p>
<p>Soon thereafter, Drake hosted a "<a href="/wiki/Search_for_extraterrestrial_intelligence" title="Search for extraterrestrial intelligence">search for extraterrestrial intelligence</a>" meeting on detecting their radio signals. The meeting was held at the Green Bank facility in 1961. The equation that bears Drake's name arose out of his preparations for the meeting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote>
<p>As I planned the meeting, I realized a few day[s] ahead of time we needed an agenda. And so I wrote down all the things you needed to know to predict how hard it's going to be to detect extraterrestrial life. And looking at them it became pretty evident that if you multiplied all these together, you got a number, N, which is the number of detectable civilizations in our galaxy. This was aimed at the radio search, and not to search for primordial or primitive life forms. —Frank Drake.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>The ten attendees were conference organizer J. Peter Pearman, Frank Drake, <a href="/wiki/Philip_Morrison" title="Philip Morrison">Philip Morrison</a>, businessman and radio amateur Dana Atchley, chemist <a href="/wiki/Melvin_Calvin" title="Melvin Calvin">Melvin Calvin</a>, astronomer <a href="/wiki/Su-Shu_Huang" title="Su-Shu Huang">Su-Shu Huang</a>, neuroscientist <a href="/wiki/John_C._Lilly" title="John C. Lilly">John C. Lilly</a>, inventor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barney_Oliver" title="Barney Oliver">Barney Oliver</a>, astronomer <a href="/wiki/Carl_Sagan" title="Carl Sagan">Carl Sagan</a> and radio-astronomer <a href="/wiki/Otto_Struve" title="Otto Struve">Otto Struve</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wende_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wende-9">[9]</a></sup> These participants dubbed themselves "The Order of the Dolphin" (because of Lilly's work on dolphin communication), and commemorated their first meeting with a plaque at the observatory hall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Equation">Equation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Drake_equation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Equation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Drake equation is:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>N</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<msub>
<mi>R</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>∗<!-- ∗ --></mo>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>⋅<!-- ⋅ --></mo>
<msub>
<mi>f</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>p</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>⋅<!-- ⋅ --></mo>
<msub>
<mi>n</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>e</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>⋅<!-- ⋅ --></mo>
<msub>
<mi>f</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>ℓ<!-- ℓ --></mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>⋅<!-- ⋅ --></mo>
<msub>
<mi>f</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>i</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>⋅<!-- ⋅ --></mo>
<msub>
<mi>f</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>c</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>⋅<!-- ⋅ --></mo>
<mi>L</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle N=R_{\ast }\cdot f_{p}\cdot n_{e}\cdot f_{\ell }\cdot f_{i}\cdot f_{c}\cdot L}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="N=R_{\ast }\cdot f_{p}\cdot n_{e}\cdot f_{\ell }\cdot f_{i}\cdot f_{c}\cdot L" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/df83ab73eacd94fd43a9136a2e7352b70e32fc5d" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:30.51ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>where:</p>
<dl>
<dd><i>N</i> = the number of <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">civilizations</a> in our galaxy with which communication might be possible (i.e. which are on our current past <a href="/wiki/Light_cone" title="Light cone">light cone</a>);</dd>
</dl>
<p>and</p>
<dl>
<dd><i>R</i><sub>*</sub> = the average rate of <a href="/wiki/Star_formation" title="Star formation">star formation</a> in <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">our galaxy</a></dd>
<dd><i>f</i><sub><i>p</i></sub> = the fraction of those stars that have <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a></dd>
<dd><i>n</i><sub><i>e</i></sub> = the average number of planets that can potentially support <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a> per star that has planets</dd>
<dd><i>f</i><sub><i>l</i></sub> = the fraction of planets that could support life that actually develop life at some point</dd>
<dd><i>f</i><sub><i>i</i></sub> = the fraction of planets with life that actually go on to develop <a href="/wiki/Intelligence" title="Intelligence">intelligent</a> life (civilizations)</dd>
<dd><i>f</i><sub><i>c</i></sub> = the fraction of civilizations that develop a technology that releases detectable signs of their existence into space</dd>
<dd><i>L</i> = the length of time for which such civilizations release detectable signals into space<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOVA_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-NOVA-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SETI-Drake-Equation_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-SETI-Drake-Equation-13">[13]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Usefulness">Usefulness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Drake_equation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Usefulness">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:C_G-K_-_DSC_0421.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="408" height="270" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/C_G-K_-_DSC_0421.jpg/220px-C_G-K_-_DSC_0421.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/C_G-K_-_DSC_0421.jpg/330px-C_G-K_-_DSC_0421.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0c/C_G-K_-_DSC_0421.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:C_G-K_-_DSC_0421.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Allen_Telescope_Array" title="Allen Telescope Array">Allen Telescope Array</a> for SETI</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Drake equation amounts to a summary of the factors affecting the likelihood that we might detect radio-communication from intelligent extraterrestrial life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burchell_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Burchell-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOVA_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-NOVA-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> The last four parameters, <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>f</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>ℓ<!-- ℓ --></mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>,</mo>
<msub>
<mi>f</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>i</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>,</mo>
<msub>
<mi>f</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>c</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle f_{\ell },f_{i},f_{c},}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="f_{\ell}, f_i, f_c," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c72ec22c8c8b3ce7f205abd6c4d0253061ddf6c3" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:8.879ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> and <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>L</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle L}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="L" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/103168b86f781fe6e9a4a87b8ea1cebe0ad4ede8" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.593ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>, are not known and are very hard to estimate, with values ranging over many orders of magnitude (see <a href="#Criticism">criticism</a>). Therefore, the usefulness of the Drake equation is not in the solving, but rather in the contemplation of all the various concepts which scientists must incorporate when considering the question of life elsewhere,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burchell_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-Burchell-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-December_2002_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-December_2002-3">[3]</a></sup> and gives the question of life elsewhere a basis for <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific analysis</a>. The Drake equation is a statement that stimulates intellectual curiosity about the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a> around us, for helping us to understand that <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a> as we know it is the end product of a natural, cosmic evolution, and for helping us realize how much we are a part of that universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SETI-Drake-Equation_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-SETI-Drake-Equation-13">[13]</a></sup> What the equation and the search for life has done is focus science on some of the other questions about life in the universe, specifically <a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis" title="Abiogenesis">abiogenesis</a>, the development of multi-cellular life and the development of <a href="/wiki/Intelligence" title="Intelligence">intelligence</a> itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>Within the limits of our existing technology, any practical search for distant intelligent life must necessarily be a search for some manifestation of a distant technology. After about 50 years, the Drake equation is still of seminal importance because it is a 'road map' of what we need to learn in order to solve this fundamental existential question.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burchell_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-Burchell-1">[1]</a></sup> It also formed the backbone of <a href="/wiki/Astrobiology" title="Astrobiology">astrobiology</a> as a science; although speculation is entertained to give context, astrobiology concerns itself primarily with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypotheses" title="Hypotheses">hypotheses</a> that fit firmly into existing <a href="/wiki/Theory#Science" title="Theory">scientific theories</a>. Some 50 years of SETI have failed to find anything, even though radio telescopes, receiver techniques, and computational abilities have improved enormously since the early 1960s, but it has been discovered, at least, that our galaxy is not teeming with very powerful alien transmitters continuously broadcasting near the 21 cm hydrogen frequency. No one could say this in 1961.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Modifications">Modifications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Drake_equation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Modifications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>As many observers have pointed out, the Drake equation is a very simple model that does not include potentially relevant parameters,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> and many changes and modifications to the equation have been proposed. One line of modification, for example, attempts to account for the uncertainty inherent in many of the terms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>Others note that the Drake equation ignores many concepts that might be relevant to the odds of contacting other civilizations. For example, <a href="/wiki/David_Brin" title="David Brin">David Brin</a> states: "The Drake equation merely speaks of the number of sites at which ETIs spontaneously arise. The equation says nothing directly about the contact cross-section between an ETIS and contemporary human society".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GS_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-GS-19">[19]</a></sup> Because it is the contact cross-section that is of interest to the SETI community, many additional factors and modifications of the Drake equation have been proposed.</p>
<dl>
<dt>Colonization</dt>
</dl>
<p>It has been proposed to generalize the Drake equation to include additional effects of alien civilizations colonizing other <a href="/wiki/Star_system" title="Star system">star systems</a>. Each original site expands with an expansion velocity <i>v</i>, and establishes additional sites that survive for a lifetime <i>L</i>. The result is a more complex set of 3 equations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GS_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-GS-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dt>Reappearance factor</dt>
</dl>
<p>The Drake equation may furthermore be multiplied by <i>how many times</i> an intelligent civilization may occur on planets where it has happened once. Even if an intelligent civilization reaches the end of its lifetime after, for example, 10,000 years, life may still prevail on the planet for billions of years, permitting the next <a href="/wiki/Sociocultural_evolution" title="Sociocultural evolution">civilization to evolve</a>. Thus, several civilizations may come and go during the lifespan of one and the same planet. Thus, if <i>n</i><sub>r</sub> is the average number of times a new civilization reappears on the same planet where a previous civilization once has appeared and ended, then the total number of civilizations on such a planet would be (1+<i>n</i><sub>r</sub>), which is the actual <i>reappearance factor</i> added to the equation.</p>
<p>The factor depends on what generally is the cause of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civilization_extinction" title="Civilization extinction">civilization extinction</a>. If it is generally by temporary uninhabitability, for example a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_winter" title="Nuclear winter">nuclear winter</a>, then <i>n</i><sub>r</sub> may be relatively high. On the other hand, if it is generally by permanent uninhabitability, such as <a href="/wiki/Stellar_evolution" title="Stellar evolution">stellar evolution</a>, then <i>n</i><sub>r</sub> may be almost zero. In the case of total life extinction, a similar factor may be applicable for <i>f</i><sub>ℓ</sub>, that is, <i>how many times</i> life may appear on a planet where it has appeared once.</p>
<dl>
<dt>METI factor</dt>
</dl>
<p><a href="/wiki/Aleksandr_Leonidovich_Zaitsev" title="Aleksandr Leonidovich Zaitsev">Alexander Zaitsev</a> said that to be in a communicative phase and emit dedicated messages are not the same. For example, humans, although being in a communicative phase, are not a communicative civilization; we do not practise such activities as the purposeful and regular transmission of interstellar messages. For this reason, he suggested introducing the METI factor (Messaging to Extra-Terrestrial Intelligence) to the classical Drake equation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> He defined the factor as "the fraction of communicative civilizations with clear and non-paranoid planetary consciousness", or alternatively expressed, the fraction of communicative civilizations that actually engage in deliberate interstellar transmission.</p>
<p>The METI factor is somewhat misleading since active, purposeful transmission of messages by a civilization is not required for them to receive a broadcast sent by another that is seeking first contact. It is merely required they have capable and compatible receiver systems operational; however, this is a variable humans cannot accurately estimate.</p>
<dl>
<dt>Biogenic gases</dt>
</dl>
<p>Astronomer <a href="/wiki/Sara_Seager" title="Sara Seager">Sara Seager</a> proposed a revised equation that focuses on the search for planets with biosignature gases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20161207_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20161207-21">[21]</a></sup> These gases are produced by living organisms that can accumulate in a planet atmosphere to levels that can be detected with remote space telescopes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-equation_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-equation-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Seager equation looks like this:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-equation_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-equation-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[a]</a></sup> <div><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>N</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<msub>
<mi>N</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>∗<!-- ∗ --></mo>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>∗<!-- ∗ --></mo>
<msub>
<mi>F</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>Q</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>∗<!-- ∗ --></mo>
<msub>
<mi>F</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mtext>HZ</mtext>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>∗<!-- ∗ --></mo>
<msub>
<mi>F</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>O</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>∗<!-- ∗ --></mo>
<msub>
<mi>F</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>L</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>∗<!-- ∗ --></mo>
<msub>
<mi>F</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>S</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle N=N_{*}*F_{Q}*F_{\text{HZ}}*F_{O}*F_{L}*F_{S}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle N = N_**F_Q*F_\text{HZ}*F_O*F_L*F_S}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a640308cf4b24c1ef34fa5cae6cfadfb9fb7ba6c" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:34.848ex; height:2.843ex;"/></div> Where:</p>
<dl>
<dd>N = the number of planets with detectable signs of life</dd>
<dd>N<sub>*</sub> = the number of stars observed</dd>
<dd>F<sub>Q</sub> = the fraction of stars that are quiet</dd>
<dd>F<sub>HZ</sub> = the fraction of stars with rocky planets in the habitable zone</dd>
<dd>F<sub>O</sub> = the fraction of those planets that can be observed</dd>
<dd>F<sub>L</sub> = the fraction that have life</dd>
<dd>F<sub>S</sub> = the fraction on which life produces a detectable signature gas</dd>
</dl>
<p>Seager stresses, “We’re not throwing out the Drake Equation, which is really a different topic,” explaining, “Since Drake came up with the equation, we have discovered thousands of exoplanets. We as a community have had our views revolutionized as to what could possibly be out there. And now we have a real question on our hands, one that’s not related to intelligent life: Can we detect any signs of life in any way in the very near future?”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Estimates">Estimates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Drake_equation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Estimates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Original_estimates">Original estimates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Drake_equation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Original estimates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There is considerable disagreement on the values of these parameters, but the 'educated guesses' used by Drake and his colleagues in 1961 were:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><i>R</i><sub>*</sub> = 1/year (1 star formed per year, on the average over the life of the galaxy; this was regarded as conservative)</li>
<li><i>f</i><sub>p</sub> = 0.2-0.5 (one fifth to one half of all stars formed will have planets)</li>
<li><i>n</i><sub>e</sub> = 1-5 (stars with planets will have between 1 and 5 planets capable of developing life)</li>
<li><i>f</i><sub>l</sub> = 1 (100% of these planets will develop life)</li>
<li><i>f</i><sub>i</sub> = 1 (100% of which will develop intelligent life)</li>
<li><i>f</i><sub>c</sub> = 0.1-0.2 (10-20% of which will be able to communicate)</li>
<li><i>L</i> = 1000-100,000,000 years (which will last somewhere between 1000 and 100,000,000 years)</li>
</ul>
<p>Inserting the above minimum numbers into the equation gives a minimum N of 20. Inserting the maximum numbers gives a maximum of 50,000,000. Drake states that given the uncertainties, the original meeting concluded that <i>N</i> ≈ <i>L</i>, and there were probably between 1000 and 100,000,000 civilizations in the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a> galaxy.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_estimates">Current estimates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Drake_equation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Current estimates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>This section discusses and attempts to list the best current estimates for the parameters of the Drake equation.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Rate_of_star_creation_in_our_galaxy.2C_R.2A">Rate of star creation in our galaxy, <i>R</i><sub>*</sub></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Drake_equation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Rate of star creation in our galaxy, R*">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<dl>
<dd>Latest calculations from <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> and the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a> indicate that the current rate of star formation in our galaxy is about 0.68–1.45 <a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass"><i>M</i><sub>☉</sub></a> of material per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robitaille_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Robitaille-27">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Drake_Equation_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Drake_Equation-28">[27]</a></sup> To get the number of stars per year, this must account for the <a href="/wiki/Initial_mass_function" title="Initial mass function">initial mass function</a> (IMF) for stars, where the average new star mass is about 0.5 <i>M</i><sub>☉</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup> This gives a star formation rate of about 1.5-3 stars per year.</dd>
</dl>
<p><span id="eta-earth"></span></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fraction_of_those_stars_that_have_planets.2C_fp">Fraction of those stars that have planets, <i>f</i><sub>p</sub></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Drake_equation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Fraction of those stars that have planets, fp">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<dl>
<dd>Recent analysis of <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_microlensing" title="Gravitational microlensing">microlensing</a> surveys has found that <i>f</i><sub>p</sub> may approach 1—that is, stars are orbited by planets as a rule, rather than the exception; and that there are one or more bound planets per Milky Way star.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc.co.uk_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.co.uk-30">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nature-20120111_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nature-20120111-31">[30]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Average_number_of_planets_per_star_having_planets_that_might_support_life.2C_ne">Average number of planets per star having planets that might support life, <i>n</i><sub>e</sub></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Drake_equation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Average number of planets per star having planets that might support life, ne">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<dl>
<dd>In November 2013, astronomers reported, based on <a href="/wiki/Kepler_(spacecraft)" title="Kepler (spacecraft)"><i>Kepler</i> space mission</a> data, that there could be as many as 40 billion <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">Earth-sized</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extrasolar_planets" title="Extrasolar planets">planets</a> orbiting in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habitable_zone" title="Habitable zone">habitable zones</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sun-like" title="Sun-like">sun-like stars</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_dwarf_stars" title="Red dwarf stars">red dwarf stars</a> within the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milky_Way_Galaxy" title="Milky Way Galaxy">Milky Way Galaxy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20131104_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20131104-32">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PNAS-20131031_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-PNAS-20131031-33">[32]</a></sup> 11 billion of these estimated planets may be orbiting sun-like stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LATimes-20131104_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-LATimes-20131104-34">[33]</a></sup> Since there are about 100 billion stars in the galaxy, this implies <i>f</i><sub>p</sub>*<i>n</i><sub>e</sub> is roughly 0.4. The nearest planet in the habitable zone may be as little as 12 light-years away, according to the scientists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20131104_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20131104-32">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PNAS-20131031_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-PNAS-20131031-33">[32]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd>The consensus at the Green Bank meeting was that <i>n</i><sub>e</sub> had a minimum value between three and five. Dutch astronomer <a href="/wiki/Govert_Schilling" title="Govert Schilling">Govert Schilling</a> has opined that this is optimistic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schilling2011_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-schilling2011-35">[34]</a></sup> Even if planets are in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habitable_zone" title="Habitable zone">habitable zone</a>, the number of planets with the right proportion of elements is difficult to estimate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trimble_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Trimble-36">[35]</a></sup> Brad Gibson, Yeshe Fenner, and Charley Lineweaver determined that about 10% of <a href="/wiki/Star_system" title="Star system">star systems</a> in the Milky Way galaxy are hospitable to life, by having heavy elements, being far from <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernovae</a> and being stable for a sufficient time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd>The discovery of numerous <a href="/wiki/Gas_giant" title="Gas giant">gas giants</a> in close orbit with their stars has introduced doubt that life-supporting planets commonly survive the formation of their stellar systems. So-called <a href="/wiki/Hot_Jupiter" title="Hot Jupiter">hot Jupiters</a> may migrate from distant orbits to near orbits, in the process disrupting the orbits of habitable planets.</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd>On the other hand, the variety of <a href="/wiki/Star_system" title="Star system">star systems</a> that might have habitable zones is not just limited to solar-type stars and Earth-sized planets. It is now estimated that even tidally locked planets close to <a href="/wiki/Red_dwarf" title="Red dwarf">red dwarf</a> stars <a href="/wiki/Habitability_of_red_dwarf_systems" title="Habitability of red dwarf systems">might have habitable zones</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup> although the flaring behavior of these stars might argue against this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup> The possibility of life on <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">moons</a> of gas giants (such as <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>'s moon <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>'s moon <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a>) adds further uncertainty to this figure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd>The authors of the <a href="/wiki/Rare_Earth_hypothesis" title="Rare Earth hypothesis">rare Earth hypothesis</a> propose a number of additional constraints on habitability for planets, including being in galactic zones with suitably low radiation, high star metallicity, and low enough density to avoid excessive asteroid bombardment. They also propose that it is necessary to have a planetary system with large gas giants which provide bombardment protection without a <a href="/wiki/Hot_Jupiter" title="Hot Jupiter">hot Jupiter</a>; and a planet with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plate_tectonic" title="Plate tectonic">plate tectonics</a>, a large moon that creates tidal pools, and moderate <a href="/wiki/Axial_tilt" title="Axial tilt">axial tilt</a> to generate seasonal variation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RareEarth_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-RareEarth-41">[40]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fraction_of_the_above_that_actually_go_on_to_develop_life.2C_fl">Fraction of the above that actually go on to develop life, <i>f</i><sub>l</sub></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Drake_equation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Fraction of the above that actually go on to develop life, fl">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<dl>
<dd>Geological evidence from the Earth suggests that <i>f</i><sub>l</sub> may be high; life on Earth appears to have begun around the same time as favorable conditions arose, suggesting that <a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis" title="Abiogenesis">abiogenesis</a> may be relatively common once conditions are right. However, this evidence only looks at the Earth (a single model planet), and contains <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anthropic_bias" title="Anthropic bias">anthropic bias</a>, as the planet of study was not chosen randomly, but by the living organisms that already inhabit it (ourselves). From a classical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypothesis_testing" title="Hypothesis testing">hypothesis testing</a> standpoint, there are zero <a href="/wiki/Degrees_of_freedom_(statistics)" title="Degrees of freedom (statistics)">degrees of freedom</a>, permitting no valid estimates to be made. If life were to be found on <a href="/wiki/Life_on_Mars" title="Life on Mars">Mars</a> that developed independently from life on Earth it would imply a value for <i>f</i><sub>l</sub> close to one. While this would raise the degrees of freedom from zero to one, there would remain a great deal of uncertainty on any estimate due to the small sample size, and the chance they are not really independent.</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd>Countering this argument is that there is no evidence for abiogenesis occurring more than once on the Earth — that is, all terrestrial life stems from a common origin. If abiogenesis were more common it would be speculated to have occurred more than once on the Earth. Scientists have searched for this by looking for <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> that are unrelated to other life on Earth, but none have been found yet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup> It is also possible that life arose more than once, but that other branches were out-competed, or died in mass extinctions, or were lost in other ways. Biochemists <a href="/wiki/Francis_Crick" title="Francis Crick">Francis Crick</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leslie_Orgel" title="Leslie Orgel">Leslie Orgel</a> laid special emphasis on this uncertainty: "At the moment we have no means at all of knowing" whether we are "likely to be alone in the galaxy (Universe)" or whether "the galaxy may be pullulating with life of many different forms."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup> As an alternative to abiogenesis on Earth, they proposed the hypothesis of <a href="/wiki/Directed_panspermia" title="Directed panspermia">directed panspermia</a>, which states that Earth life began with "microorganisms sent here deliberately by a technological society on another planet, by means of a special long-range unmanned spaceship".</dd>
</dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fraction_of_the_above_that_develops_intelligent_life.2C_fi">Fraction of the above that develops intelligent life, <i>f</i><sub>i</sub></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Drake_equation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Fraction of the above that develops intelligent life, fi">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<dl>
<dd>This value remains particularly controversial. Those who favor a low value, such as the biologist <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Mayr" title="Ernst Mayr">Ernst Mayr</a>, point out that of the billions of species that have existed on Earth, only one has become intelligent and from this, infer a tiny value for <i>f</i><sub>i</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ernst_Mayr_on_SETI_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ernst_Mayr_on_SETI-44">[43]</a></sup> Likewise, the Rare Earth hypothesis, notwithstanding their low value for <i>n</i><sub>e</sub> above, also think a low value for <i>f</i><sub>i</sub> dominates the analysis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup> Those who favor higher values note the generally increasing complexity of life over time, concluding that the appearance of intelligence is almost inevitable,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acampbell.ukfsn.org_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-acampbell.ukfsn.org-46">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup> implying an <i>f</i><sub>i</sub> approaching 1. Skeptics point out that the large spread of values in this factor and others make all estimates unreliable. (See <a href="#Criticism">Criticism</a>).</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd>In addition, while it appears that life developed soon after the formation of Earth, the <a href="/wiki/Cambrian_explosion" title="Cambrian explosion">Cambrian explosion</a>, in which a large variety of multicellular life forms came into being, occurred a considerable amount of time after the formation of Earth, which suggests the possibility that special conditions were necessary. Some scenarios such as the <a href="/wiki/Snowball_Earth" title="Snowball Earth">Snowball Earth</a> or research into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extinction_events" title="Extinction events">extinction events</a> have raised the possibility that life on Earth is relatively fragile. Research on any past <a href="/wiki/Life_on_Mars" title="Life on Mars">life on Mars</a> is relevant since a discovery that life did form on Mars but ceased to exist might raise our estimate of f<sub>l</sub> but would indicate that in half the known cases, intelligent life did not develop.</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd>This model also has a large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anthropic_bias" title="Anthropic bias">anthropic bias</a> and there are still zero <a href="/wiki/Degrees_of_freedom_(statistics)" title="Degrees of freedom (statistics)">degrees of freedom</a>. Note that the capacity and willingness to participate in extraterrestrial communication has come relatively recently, with the Earth having only an estimated 100,000 year history of intelligent human life, and less than a century of technological ability.</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd>Estimates of <i>f</i><sub>i</sub> have been affected by discoveries that the Solar System's orbit is circular in the galaxy, at such a distance that it remains out of the spiral arms for tens of millions of years (evading radiation from <a href="/wiki/Nova" title="Nova">novae</a>). Also, Earth's large moon may aid the evolution of life by <a href="/wiki/Rare_Earth_hypothesis#A_large_moon" title="Rare Earth hypothesis">stabilizing the planet's axis of rotation</a>.</dd>
</dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fraction_of_the_above_revealing_their_existence_via_signal_release_into_space.2C_fc">Fraction of the above revealing their existence via signal release into space, <i>f</i><sub>c</sub></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Drake_equation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Fraction of the above revealing their existence via signal release into space, fc">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<dl>
<dd>For deliberate communication, the one example we have (the Earth) does not do much explicit communication, though there are <a href="/wiki/Active_SETI" title="Active SETI">some efforts</a> covering only a tiny fraction of the stars that might look for our presence. (See <a href="/wiki/Arecibo_message" title="Arecibo message">Arecibo message</a>, for example). There is <a href="/wiki/Fermi_paradox#They_choose_not_to_interact_with_us" title="Fermi paradox">considerable speculation</a> why an extraterrestrial civilization might exist but choose not to communicate. However, deliberate communication is not required, and calculations indicate that current or near-future Earth-level technology might well be detectable to civilizations not too much more advanced than our own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup> By this standard, the Earth is a communicating civilization.</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd>Another question is what percentage of civilizations in the galaxy are close enough for us to detect, assuming that they send out signals. For example, existing Earth radio telescopes could only detect Earth radio transmissions from roughly a light year away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Lifetime_of_such_a_civilization_wherein_it_communicates_its_signals_into_space.2C_L">Lifetime of such a civilization wherein it communicates its signals into space, <i>L</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Drake_equation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Lifetime of such a civilization wherein it communicates its signals into space, L">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<dl>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Michael_Shermer" title="Michael Shermer">Michael Shermer</a> estimated <i>L</i> as 420 years, based on the duration of sixty historical Earthly civilizations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Why_ET_Hasn.E2.80.99t_Called_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Why_ET_Hasn.E2.80.99t_Called-50">[49]</a></sup> Using 28 civilizations more recent than the Roman Empire, he calculates a figure of 304 years for "modern" civilizations. It could also be argued from Michael Shermer's results that the fall of most of these civilizations was followed by later civilizations that carried on the technologies, so it is doubtful that they are separate civilizations in the context of the Drake equation. In the expanded version, including <i>reappearance number</i>, this lack of specificity in defining single civilizations does not matter for the end result, since such a civilization turnover could be described as an increase in the <i>reappearance number</i> rather than increase in <i>L</i>, stating that a civilization reappears in the form of the succeeding cultures. Furthermore, since none could communicate over interstellar space, the method of comparing with historical civilizations could be regarded as invalid.</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd><a href="/wiki/David_Grinspoon" title="David Grinspoon">David Grinspoon</a> has argued that once a civilization has developed enough, it might overcome all threats to its survival. It will then last for an indefinite period of time, making the value for <i>L</i> potentially billions of years. If this is the case, then he proposes that the Milky Way galaxy may have been steadily accumulating advanced civilizations since it formed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-David_Grinspoon_2004_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-David_Grinspoon_2004-51">[50]</a></sup> He proposes that the last factor <i>L</i> be replaced with <i>f</i><sub>IC</sub>*<i>T</i>, where <i>f</i><sub>IC</sub> is the fraction of communicating civilizations become "immortal" (in the sense that they simply do not die out), and <i>T</i> representing the length of time during which this process has been going on. This has the advantage that <i>T</i> would be a relatively easy to discover number, as it would simply be some fraction of the age of the universe.</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd>It has also been hypothesized that once a civilization has learned of a more advanced one, its longevity could increase because it can learn from the experiences of the other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GoldsmithOwen_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-GoldsmithOwen-52">[51]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd>The astronomer <a href="/wiki/Carl_Sagan" title="Carl Sagan">Carl Sagan</a> speculated that all of the terms, except for the lifetime of a civilization, are relatively high and the determining factor in whether there are large or small numbers of civilizations in the universe is the civilization lifetime, or in other words, the ability of technological civilizations to avoid self-destruction. In Sagan's case, the Drake equation was a strong motivating factor for his interest in environmental issues and his efforts to warn against the dangers of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_warfare" title="Nuclear warfare">nuclear warfare</a>.</dd>
</dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Range_of_results">Range of results</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Drake_equation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Range of results">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As many skeptics have pointed out, the Drake equation can give a very wide range of values, depending on the assumptions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup> and the values used in portions of the Drake equation are not well-established.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schilling2011_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-schilling2011-35">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-renamed_from_2011_on_20160211035411_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-renamed_from_2011_on_20160211035411-54">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schilling2009_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-schilling2009-56">[55]</a></sup> In particular, the result can be <i>N</i> ≪ 1, meaning we are likely alone in the galaxy, or <i>N</i> ≫ 1, implying there are many civilizations we might contact. One of the few points of wide agreement is that the presence of humanity implies a probability of intelligence arising of greater than zero.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dean_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dean-57">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>As an example of a low estimate, combining NASA's star formation rates, the <a href="/wiki/Rare_Earth_hypothesis" title="Rare Earth hypothesis">rare Earth hypothesis</a> value of <i>f</i><sub>p</sub>*<i>n</i><sub>e</sub>*<i>f</i><sub>l</sub> = 10<sup>−5</sup>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup> Mayr's view on intelligence arising, Drake's view of communication, and Shermer's's estimate of lifetime:</p>
<dl>
<dd><i>R</i><sub>*</sub> = 1.5-3/year,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robitaille_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Robitaille-27">[26]</a></sup> <i>f</i><sub>p</sub>*<i>n</i><sub>e</sub>*<i>f</i><sub>l</sub> = 10<sup>−5</sup>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RareEarth_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-RareEarth-41">[40]</a></sup> <i>f</i><sub>i</sub> = 10<sup>−9</sup>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ernst_Mayr_on_SETI_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ernst_Mayr_on_SETI-44">[43]</a></sup> <i>f</i><sub>c</sub> = 0.2<sup>[Drake, above]</sup>, and <i>L</i> = 304 years<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Why_ET_Hasn.E2.80.99t_Called_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-Why_ET_Hasn.E2.80.99t_Called-50">[49]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>gives:</p>
<dl>
<dd><i>N</i> = 1.5 × 10<sup>−5</sup> × 10<sup>−9</sup> × 0.2 × 304 = 9.1 x 10<sup>−11</sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>i.e., suggesting that we are probably alone in this galaxy, and possibly the observable universe.</p>
<p>On the other hand, with larger values for each of the parameters above, values of <i>N</i> can be derived that are greater than 1. The following higher values that have been proposed for each of the parameters:</p>
<dl>
<dd><i>R</i><sub>*</sub> = 1.5-3/year,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robitaille_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-Robitaille-27">[26]</a></sup> <i>f</i><sub>p</sub> = 1,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc.co.uk_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.co.uk-30">[29]</a></sup> <i>n</i><sub>e</sub> = 0.2,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-W._von_Bloh.2C_C.Bounama.2C_M._Cuntz.2C_and_S._Franck._2007_1365_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-W._von_Bloh.2C_C.Bounama.2C_M._Cuntz.2C_and_S._Franck._2007_1365-59">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-F._Selsis.2C_J.F._Kasting.2C_B._Levrard.2C_J._Paillet.2C_I._Ribas.2C_and_X._Delfosse._2007_1373_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-F._Selsis.2C_J.F._Kasting.2C_B._Levrard.2C_J._Paillet.2C_I._Ribas.2C_and_X._Delfosse._2007_1373-60">[59]</a></sup> <i>f</i><sub>l</sub> = 0.13,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lineweaver.2C_C._H._.26_Davis.2C_T._M._2002_293.E2.80.93304_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lineweaver.2C_C._H._.26_Davis.2C_T._M._2002_293.E2.80.93304-61">[60]</a></sup> <i>f</i><sub>i</sub> = 1,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acampbell.ukfsn.org_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-acampbell.ukfsn.org-46">[45]</a></sup> <i>f</i><sub>c</sub> = 0.2<sup>[Drake, above]</sup>, and <i>L</i> = 10<sup>9</sup> years<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-David_Grinspoon_2004_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-David_Grinspoon_2004-51">[50]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>Use of these parameters gives:</p>
<dl>
<dd><i>N</i> = 3 × 1 × 0.2 × 0.13 × 1 × 0.2 × 10<sup>9</sup> = 156 million.</dd>
</dl>
<p><a href="/wiki/Monte_Carlo_method" title="Monte Carlo method">Monte Carlo</a> simulations of estimates of the Drake equation factors based on a stellar and planetary model of the Milky Way have resulted in the number of civilizations varying by a factor of 100.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Has_intelligent_life_ever_existed.3F">Has intelligent life <i>ever</i> existed?</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Drake_equation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Has intelligent life ever existed?">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Drake equation can be modified to determine just how unlikely intelligent life must be, to give the result that Earth has the only intelligent life that has ever arisen, either in our galaxy or the universe as a whole. This simplifies the calculation by removing the lifetime and communication constraints. Since star and planets counts are known, this leaves the only unknown as the odds that a habitable planet <i>ever</i> develops intelligent life. For Earth to have the only civilization that has ever occurred in the universe, then the odds of any habitable planet ever developing such a civilization must be less than <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6976250000000000000♠</span>2.5<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−24</sup></span>. Similarly, for Earth to host the only civilization in our galaxy for all time, the odds of a habitable zone planet ever hosting intelligent life must be less than <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6989170000000000000♠</span>1.7<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−11</sup></span> (about 1 in 60 billion). The figure for the universe implies that it is highly unlikely that Earth hosts the only intelligent life that has ever occurred. The figure for our galaxy suggests that other civilizations may have occurred or will likely occur in our galaxy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">Criticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Drake_equation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Criticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Criticism of the Drake equation follows mostly from the observation that several terms in the equation are largely or entirely based on conjecture. Star formation rates are well-known, and the incidence of planets has a sound theoretical and observational basis, but the other terms in the equation become very speculative. The uncertainties revolve around our understanding of the evolution of life, intelligence, and civilization, not physics. No statistical estimates are possible for some of the parameters, where only one example is known. The net result is that the equation cannot be used to draw firm conclusions of any kind, and the resulting margin of error is huge, far beyond what some consider acceptable or meaningful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>One reply to such criticisms<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup> is that even though the Drake equation currently involves speculation about unmeasured parameters, it was intended as a way to stimulate dialogue on these topics. Then the focus becomes how to proceed experimentally. Indeed, Drake originally formulated the equation merely as an agenda for discussion at the Green Bank conference.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fermi_paradox">Fermi paradox</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Drake_equation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Fermi paradox">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fermi_paradox" title="Fermi paradox">Fermi paradox</a></div>
<p>The pessimists' most telling argument in the SETI debate stems not from theory or conjecture but from an actual observation: the presumed lack of extraterrestrial contact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-history-6">[6]</a></sup> A civilization lasting for tens of millions of years would have plenty of time to travel anywhere in the galaxy, even at the slow speeds <a href="/wiki/100_Year_Starship" title="100 Year Starship">foreseeable with our own kind of technology</a>. Furthermore, no confirmed signs of intelligence elsewhere have been recognized as such, either in our galaxy or in the <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe" title="Observable universe">observable universe</a> of 2 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trillion_(short_scale)" title="Trillion (short scale)">trillion</a> galaxies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Conselice_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Conselice-71">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20161017_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20161017-72">[71]</a></sup> According to this line of thinking, the tendency to fill up all available territory seems to be a universal trait of living things, so the Earth should have already been colonized, or at least visited, but no evidence of this exists. Hence Fermi's question "Where is everybody?".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OSTI-19850301_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-OSTI-19850301-73">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>A large number of explanations have been proposed to explain this lack of contact; a book published in 2015 elaborated on 75 different explanations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup> In terms of the Drake Equation, the explanations can be divided into three classes:</p>
<ul>
<li>Few intelligent civilizations ever arise. This is an argument that at least one of the first few terms, <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msup>
<mi>R</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>∗<!-- ∗ --></mo>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>⋅<!-- ⋅ --></mo>
<msub>
<mi>f</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>p</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>⋅<!-- ⋅ --></mo>
<msub>
<mi>n</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>e</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>⋅<!-- ⋅ --></mo>
<msub>
<mi>f</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>ℓ<!-- ℓ --></mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>⋅<!-- ⋅ --></mo>
<msub>
<mi>f</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>i</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle R^{\ast }\cdot f_{p}\cdot n_{e}\cdot f_{\ell }\cdot f_{i}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="R^{\ast} \cdot f_p \cdot n_e \cdot f_{\ell} \cdot f_i " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/462c3e2a05258840160c36f2ddb8aaa19dc64b41" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:18.253ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span>, has a low value. The most common suspect is <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>f</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>i</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle f_{i}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="f_{i}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/65da883ca3d16b461e46c94777b0d9c4aa010e79" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:1.957ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>, but explanations such as the rare Earth Hypothesis argue that <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>n</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>e</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle n_{e}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="n_{e}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5e21303faca6e2167f4ecbf2a75aed982e817035" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.411ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span> is the small term.</li>
<li>Intelligent civilizations exist, but we see no evidence, meaning <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>f</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>c</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle f_{c}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="f_{c}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d2b64a46c31800830b9fecc59b22812390e18c05" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.101ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> is small. Typical arguments include <a href="/wiki/Fermi_paradox#Intelligent_civilizations_are_too_far_apart_in_space_or_time" title="Fermi paradox">that civilizations are too far apart</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fermi_paradox#It_is_too_expensive_to_spread_physically_throughout_the_galaxy" title="Fermi paradox">it is too expensive to spread throughout the galaxy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fermi_paradox#Civilizations_broadcast_detectable_radio_signals_only_for_a_brief_period_of_time" title="Fermi paradox">civilizations broadcast signals for only a brief period of time</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fermi_paradox#It_is_dangerous_to_communicate" title="Fermi paradox">it is dangerous to communicate</a>, and many others.</li>
<li>The lifetime of intelligent civilizations is short, meaning the value of <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>L</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle L}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="L" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/103168b86f781fe6e9a4a87b8ea1cebe0ad4ede8" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.593ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is small. Drake suggested that a large number of extraterrestrial civilizations would form, and he further speculated that the lack of evidence of such civilizations may be because technological civilizations tend to disappear rather quickly. Typical explanations include <a href="/wiki/Fermi_paradox#It_is_the_nature_of_intelligent_life_to_destroy_itself" title="Fermi paradox">it is the nature of intelligent life to destroy itself</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fermi_paradox#It_is_the_nature_of_intelligent_life_to_destroy_others" title="Fermi paradox">it is the nature of intelligent life to destroy others</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fermi_paradox#They_tend_to_experience_a_technological_singularity" title="Fermi paradox">they tend to experience a technological singularity</a>, and others.</li>
</ul>
<p>These lines of reasoning lead to the <a href="/wiki/Great_Filter" title="Great Filter">Great Filter</a> hypothesis,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup> which states that since there are no observed extraterrestrial civilizations, despite the vast number of stars, then some step in the process must be acting as a filter to reduce the final value. According to this view, either it is very hard for intelligent life to arise, or the lifetime of such civilizations, or the period of time they reveal their existence, must be relatively short.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_fiction_and_popular_culture">In fiction and popular culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Drake_equation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: In fiction and popular culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Frederik_Pohl" title="Frederik Pohl">Frederik Pohl</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Award_for_Best_Short_Story" title="Hugo Award for Best Short Story">Hugo Award</a>-winning short story "<a href="/wiki/Fermi_and_Frost" title="Fermi and Frost">Fermi and Frost</a>", cites a paradox as evidence for the short lifetime of technical civilizations—that is, the possibility that once a civilization develops the power to destroy itself (perhaps by <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_warfare" title="Nuclear warfare">nuclear warfare</a>), it does.</li>
<li>Optimistic results of the equation along with unobserved extraterrestrials also serves as backdrop for humorous suggestions such as <a href="/wiki/Terry_Bisson" title="Terry Bisson">Terry Bisson</a>'s classic short story "<a href="/wiki/They%27re_Made_Out_of_Meat" title="They're Made Out of Meat">They're Made Out of Meat</a>", that there are many extraterrestrial civilizations but that they are deliberately ignoring humanity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup></li>
<li>The equation was cited by <a href="/wiki/Gene_Roddenberry" title="Gene Roddenberry">Gene Roddenberry</a> as supporting the multiplicity of inhabited planets shown on <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Trek" title="Star Trek">Star Trek</a></i>, the television series he created. However, Roddenberry did not have the equation with him, and he was forced to "invent" it for his original proposal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup> The invented equation created by Roddenberry is:</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

<msup>

</dl>
</dd>
<dd>However, a number raised to the first power is merely the number itself.</dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li>Eleanor Ann Arroway paraphrases the Drake equation several times in the film <i><a href="/wiki/Contact_(1997_American_film)" title="Contact (1997 American film)">Contact</a></i> (1997), using the magnitude of <i>N</i> <sup>*</sup> and its implications on the output value to justify the SETI program.</li>
<li>In an episode of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Big_Bang_Theory_(TV_series)" title="The Big Bang Theory (TV series)">The Big Bang Theory</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Howard_Wolowitz" title="Howard Wolowitz">Howard Wolowitz</a> alludes to the Drake Equation on "Ladies Night" for the number of potential women available to the quartet, after which his friend <a href="/wiki/Sheldon_Cooper" title="Sheldon Cooper">Sheldon Cooper</a> recites the equation from memory.</li>
<li>Ambient electronic music duo <a href="/wiki/Carbon_Based_Lifeforms" title="Carbon Based Lifeforms">Carbon Based Lifeforms</a> has the Drake Equation recited in their song Abiogenesis from their album <a href="/wiki/World_of_Sleepers" title="World of Sleepers">World Of Sleepers</a>.</li>
</ul>

<li id="cite_note-58"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-58">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Rare Earth, page 270: "When we take into account factors such as the abundance of planets and the location and lifetime of the habitable zone, the Drake Equation suggests that only between 1% and 0.001% of all stars might have planets with habitats similar to Earth. [...] If microbial life forms readily, then millions to hundreds of millions of planets in the galaxy have the <i>potential</i> for developing advanced life. Springer International Publishing. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/3319132350" title="Special:BookSources/3319132350">3319132350</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADrake+equation&amp;rft.aufirst=S.&amp;rft.aulast=Webb&amp;rft.btitle=If+the+Universe+Is+Teeming+with+Aliens+...+WHERE+IS+EVERYBODY%3F%3A+Seventy-Five+Solutions+to+the+Fermi+Paradox+and+the+Problem+of+Extraterrestrial+Life&amp;rft.date=2015&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DQWKyrQEACAAJ%26dq%3DUniverse-Teeming-Aliens-WHERE-EVERYBODY&amp;rft.isbn=3319132350&amp;rft.pub=Springer+International+Publishing&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_reading">Further reading</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Drake_equation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Further reading">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li><cite class="citation book">Morton, Oliver (2002). "A Mirror in the Sky". In Graham Formelo. <i>It Must Be Beautiful</i>. Granta Books. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/1-86207-555-7" title="Special:BookSources/1-86207-555-7">1-86207-555-7</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADrake+equation&amp;rft.atitle=A+Mirror+in+the+Sky&amp;rft.aufirst=Oliver&amp;rft.aulast=Morton&amp;rft.btitle=It+Must+Be+Beautiful&amp;rft.date=2002&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.isbn=1-86207-555-7&amp;rft.pub=Granta+Books&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></li>
<li><cite class="citation book">Rood, Robert T.; James S. Trefil (1981). <i>Are We Alone? The Possibility of Extraterrestrial Civilizations</i>. New York: Scribner. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0684178427" title="Special:BookSources/0684178427">0684178427</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADrake+equation&amp;rft.aufirst=Robert+T.&amp;rft.au=James+S.+Trefil&amp;rft.aulast=Rood&amp;rft.btitle=Are+We+Alone%3F+The+Possibility+of+Extraterrestrial+Civilizations&amp;rft.date=1981&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=0684178427&amp;rft.place=New+York&amp;rft.pub=Scribner&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></li>
<li>Douglas A. Vakoch, et al.: <i>The Drake equation - estimating the prevalence of extraterrestrial life through the ages.</i> Cambridge University Press, Cambridge 2015, <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781107073654">ISBN 978-1-10-707365-4</a>.</li>
